			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><p class="mw-empty-elt"> 
</p>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Disney" redirects here. For its co-founder, see <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Disney_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Disney (disambiguation)">Disney (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<p><b>The Walt Disney Company</b>, commonly known as <b>Disney</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is an American diversified multinational mass media and entertainment <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(company)" title="Conglomerate (company)">conglomerate</a> headquartered at the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(Burbank)" title="Walt Disney Studios (Burbank)">Walt Disney Studios complex</a> in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.

data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$193.984 billion</span> (2019)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Equity_(finance)" title="Equity (finance)">Total equity</a></span></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/17px-Increase2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$93.889 billion</span> (2019)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">223,000 (September 28, 2019)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Division_(business)" title="Division (business)">Divisions</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(division)" title="Walt Disney Studios (division)">The Walt Disney Studios</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Disney_Media_Networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Disney Media Networks">Disney Media Networks</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Disney_Parks,_Experiences_and_Products" title="Disney Parks, Experiences and Products">Disney Parks, Experiences and Products</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Direct-to-Consumer_%26_International" title="Walt Disney Direct-to-Consumer &amp; International">Walt Disney Direct-to-Consumer &amp; International</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marvel_Entertainment" title="Marvel Entertainment">Marvel Entertainment</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Partners" title="National Geographic Partners">National Geographic Partners</a> (73%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TrueX" title="TrueX">TrueX</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://thewaltdisneycompany.com">thewaltdisneycompany<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span> <a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q7414#P856" title="Edit this at Wikidata"><img alt="Edit this at Wikidata" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/10px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="10" style="vertical-align: text-top" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/15px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/20px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes / references</b><br><sup id="cite_ref-annual_2018_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annual_2018-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Disney was originally founded on October 16, 1923, by brothers <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roy_O._Disney" title="Roy O. Disney">Roy O. Disney</a> as the <b>Disney Brothers Cartoon Studio</b>; it also operated under the names <b>The Walt Disney Studio</b> and <b>Walt Disney Productions</b> before officially changing its name to The Walt Disney Company in 1986. The company established itself as a leader in the <a href="/wiki/American_animation" class="mw-redirect" title="American animation">American animation</a> industry before diversifying into live-action film production, television, and theme parks.
</p><p>Since the 1980s, Disney has created and acquired corporate divisions in order to market more mature content than is typically associated with its flagship family-oriented brands. The company is known for its <a href="/wiki/Film_studio" title="Film studio">film studio</a> division, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(division)" title="Walt Disney Studios (division)">The Walt Disney Studios</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Animation Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Studios" title="Marvel Studios">Marvel Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lucasfilm" title="Lucasfilm">Lucasfilm</a>, <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Studios" title="20th Century Studios">20th Century Studios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Searchlight_Pictures" title="Searchlight Pictures">Searchlight Pictures</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blue_Sky_Studios" title="Blue Sky Studios">Blue Sky Studios</a>. Disney's other main units and reporting segments are Disney Media Networks, <a href="/wiki/Disney_Parks,_Experiences_and_Products" title="Disney Parks, Experiences and Products">Disney Parks, Experiences and Products</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Direct-to-Consumer_%26_International" title="Walt Disney Direct-to-Consumer &amp; International">Walt Disney Direct-to-Consumer &amp; International</a>. Through these segments, Disney owns and operates the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC broadcast network</a>; cable television networks such as <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">Disney Channel</a>, <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freeform_(TV_channel)" title="Freeform (TV channel)">Freeform</a>, <a href="/wiki/FX_(TV_channel)" title="FX (TV channel)">FX</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(American_TV_channel)" title="National Geographic (American TV channel)">National Geographic</a>; publishing, merchandising, music, and theater divisions; and <a href="/wiki/Disney_Parks,_Experiences_and_Products" title="Disney Parks, Experiences and Products">Disney Parks, Experiences and Products</a>, a group of 14 theme parks around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p><p>The company has been a component of the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> since 1991. Cartoon character <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a>, created in 1928 by Walt Disney and <a href="/wiki/Ub_Iwerks" title="Ub Iwerks">Ub Iwerks</a>, is one of the world's most recognizable characters and serves as the company's official mascot.
</p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_history">Corporate history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Corporate history" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="Timeline of The Walt Disney Company">Timeline of The Walt Disney Company</a></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1923.E2.80.931928:_Founding_and_silent_film_era"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1923–1928:_Founding_and_silent_film_era">1923–1928: Founding and silent film era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1923–1928: Founding and silent film era" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingswellstudio.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kingswellstudio.JPG/220px-Kingswellstudio.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3436" data-file-height="2744"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 176px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kingswellstudio.JPG/220px-Kingswellstudio.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="176" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingswellstudio.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The building in the Los Angeles neighborhood of Los Feliz which was home to the studio from 1923 to 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-MillerD_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MillerD-6">[6]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In early 1923, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City,_Missouri" title="Kansas City, Missouri">Kansas City, Missouri</a>, animator <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> created a short film entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Wonderland" title="Alice's Wonderland">Alice's Wonderland</a></i>, which featured child actress <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Davis" title="Virginia Davis">Virginia Davis</a> interacting with animated characters. After the bankruptcy in 1923 of his previous firm, <a href="/wiki/Laugh-O-Gram_Studio" title="Laugh-O-Gram Studio">Laugh-O-Gram Studio</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-cwdc_group=ChWDC_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cwdc_group=ChWDC-7">[ChWDC 1]</a></sup> Disney moved to Hollywood to join his brother, <a href="/wiki/Roy_O._Disney" title="Roy O. Disney">Roy O. Disney</a>. Film distributor <a href="/wiki/Margaret_J._Winkler" title="Margaret J. Winkler">Margaret J. Winkler</a> of M.J. Winkler Productions contacted Disney with plans to distribute a whole series of <i><a href="/wiki/Alice_Comedies" title="Alice Comedies">Alice Comedies</a></i> purchased for $1,500 per reel with Disney as a production partner. Walt and Roy Disney formed <b>Disney Brothers Cartoon Studio</b> that same year. More animated films followed after Alice.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> In January 1926, with the completion of the Disney studio on Hyperion Street, the Disney Brothers Studio's name was changed to the <b>Walt Disney Studio</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-y1926_group=ChWDC_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1926_group=ChWDC-9">[ChWDC 2]</a></sup></p><p>After the demise of the <i>Alice</i> comedies, Disney developed an all-cartoon series starring a character named <a href="/wiki/Oswald_the_Lucky_Rabbit" title="Oswald the Lucky Rabbit">Oswald the Lucky Rabbit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The series was produced by Winkler Pictures and distributed by <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Pictures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-y1926_group=ChWDC_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1926_group=ChWDC-9">[ChWDC 2]</a></sup> Universal owned Oswald, so Disney only made a few hundred dollars.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Disney completed 27 <i>Oswald</i> shorts before losing the contract in March 1928, when Winkler head <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mintz" title="Charles Mintz">Charles Mintz</a> hired away four of Disney's primary animators (the exception being <a href="/wiki/Ub_Iwerks" title="Ub Iwerks">Ub Iwerks</a>) to start his own animation studio, Snappy Comedies.<sup id="cite_ref-y1928_group=ChWDC_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1928_group=ChWDC-10">[ChWDC 3]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1928.E2.80.931934:_Mickey_Mouse_and_Silly_Symphonies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1928–1934:_Mickey_Mouse_and_Silly_Symphonies">1928–1934: Mickey Mouse and <i>Silly Symphonies</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1928–1934: Mickey Mouse and Silly Symphonies" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1928, to recover from the loss of Oswald the Lucky Rabbit, Disney came up with the idea of a mouse character named Mortimer while on a train headed to California, drawing up a few simple drawings. The mouse was later renamed <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse" title="Mickey Mouse">Mickey Mouse</a> (Disney's wife, Lillian, disliked the sound of 'Mortimer Mouse') and starred in several Disney produced films. <a href="/wiki/Ub_Iwerks" title="Ub Iwerks">Ub Iwerks</a> refined Disney's initial design of Mickey Mouse.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Disney's first sound film <i><a href="/wiki/Steamboat_Willie" title="Steamboat Willie">Steamboat Willie</a></i>, a cartoon starring Mickey, was released on November 18, 1928<sup id="cite_ref-y1928_group=ChWDC_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1928_group=ChWDC-10">[ChWDC 3]</a></sup> through <a href="/wiki/Pat_Powers_(businessman)" title="Pat Powers (businessman)">Pat Powers</a>' distribution company.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> It was the first Mickey Mouse sound cartoon released, but the third to be created, behind <i><a href="/wiki/Plane_Crazy" title="Plane Crazy">Plane Crazy</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gallopin%27_Gaucho" title="The Gallopin' Gaucho">The Gallopin' Gaucho</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-y1928_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1928-11">[ChWDC 4]</a></sup> <i>Steamboat Willie</i> was an immediate smash hit, and its initial success was attributed not just to Mickey's appeal as a character, but to the fact that it was the first cartoon to feature <a href="/wiki/Sound_film" title="Sound film">synchronized sound</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Disney used Pat Powers' Cinephone system, created by Powers using <a href="/wiki/Lee_de_Forest" title="Lee de Forest">Lee de Forest</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Phonofilm" title="Phonofilm">Phonofilm</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-y1928_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1928-11">[ChWDC 4]</a></sup> <i>Steamboat Willie</i> premiered at B. S. Moss's Colony Theater in New York City, now <a href="/wiki/Broadway_Theatre_(53rd_Street)" title="Broadway Theatre (53rd Street)">The Broadway Theatre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[8]</a></sup> Disney's <i>Plane Crazy</i> and <i>The Gallopin' Gaucho</i> were then retrofitted with synchronized sound tracks and re-released successfully in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-y1928_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1928-11">[ChWDC 4]</a></sup></p><p>Disney continued to produce cartoons with Mickey Mouse and other characters,<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> and began the <a href="/wiki/Silly_Symphony" title="Silly Symphony">Silly Symphony</a> series with <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a> signing on as Symphonies distributor in August 1929. In September 1929, theater manager Harry Woodin requested permission to start a Mickey Mouse Club, which Walt approved. In November, test comics strips were sent to <a href="/wiki/King_Features_Syndicate" title="King Features Syndicate">King Features</a>, who requested additional samples to show to the publisher, <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>. On December 16, the Walt Disney Studios partnership was reorganized as a corporation with the name of <b>Walt Disney Productions, Limited</b> with a merchandising division, Walt Disney Enterprises, and two subsidiaries, Disney Film Recording Company, Limited and Liled Realty and Investment Company for real estate holdings. Walt and his wife held 60% (6,000 shares) and Roy owned 40% of WD Productions. On December 30, King Features signed its first newspaper, <a href="/wiki/New_York_Daily_Mirror" title="New York Daily Mirror">New York Mirror</a>, to publish the <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mouse_(comic_strip)" title="Mickey Mouse (comic strip)"><i>Mickey Mouse</i> comic strip</a> with Walt's permission.<sup id="cite_ref-y1929_group=ChWDC_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1929_group=ChWDC-13">[ChWDC 5]</a></sup></p><p>In 1932, Disney signed an exclusive contract with <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a> (through the end of 1935) to produce cartoons in color, beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Flowers_and_Trees" title="Flowers and Trees">Flowers and Trees</a></i> (1932). Disney released cartoons through Powers' Celebrity Pictures (1928–1930), <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a> (1930–1932), and <a href="/wiki/United_Artists" title="United Artists">United Artists</a> (1932–1937).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[9]</a></sup> The popularity of the Mickey Mouse series allowed Disney to plan for his first feature-length animation.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The feature film <i><a href="/wiki/Walt_Before_Mickey" title="Walt Before Mickey">Walt Before Mickey</a></i>, based on the book by <a href="/wiki/Diane_Disney_Miller" title="Diane Disney Miller">Diane Disney Miller</a>, featured these moments in the studio's history.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1934.E2.80.931950:_Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs.2C_World_War_II.2C_and_package_films"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1934–1950:_Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs,_World_War_II,_and_package_films">1934–1950: <i>Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</i>, World War II, and package films</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1934–1950: Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs, World War II, and package films" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oldanimationbuilding.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Oldanimationbuilding.JPG/220px-Oldanimationbuilding.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Oldanimationbuilding.JPG/220px-Oldanimationbuilding.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oldanimationbuilding.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original Animation Building at the Walt Disney Studios.</div></div></div>
<p>Deciding to push the boundaries of animation even further, Disney began production of his first feature-length animated film in 1934. Taking three years to complete, <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</a></i>, premiered in December 1937 and by 1939 became the highest-grossing film of that time.<sup id="cite_ref-GablerBook_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GablerBook-16">[11]</a></sup> <i>Snow White</i> was released through <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Radio Pictures</a>, which had assumed distribution of Disney's product in July 1937,<sup id="cite_ref-y1937_group=ChWDC_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1937_group=ChWDC-17">[ChWDC 6]</a></sup> after United Artists attempted to attain future television rights to the Disney shorts.<sup id="cite_ref-TIME_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIME-18">[12]</a></sup> Using the profits from <i>Snow White</i>, Disney financed the construction of a new 51-acre (210,000 m<sup>2</sup>) studio complex in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank</a>, California. The new <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_(Burbank)" title="Walt Disney Studios (Burbank)">Walt Disney Studios</a>, in which the company is headquartered to this day, was completed and open for business by the end of 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-y1939_group=ChWDC_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1939_group=ChWDC-19">[ChWDC 7]</a></sup> The following year on April 2, Walt Disney Productions had its <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-y1940_group=ChWDC_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y1940_group=ChWDC-20">[ChWDC 8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[13]</a></sup></p><p>The studio continued releasing animated shorts and features, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pinocchio_(1940_film)" title="Pinocchio (1940 film)">Pinocchio</a></i> (1940), <i><a href="/wiki/Fantasia_(1940_film)" title="Fantasia (1940 film)">Fantasia</a></i> (1940), <i><a href="/wiki/Dumbo" title="Dumbo">Dumbo</a></i> (1941), and <i><a href="/wiki/Bambi" title="Bambi">Bambi</a></i> (1942).<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> After World War II began, box office profits declined. When the United States entered the war after the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>, many of Disney's animators were drafted into the armed forces. The U.S. and Canadian governments commissioned the studio <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney%27s_World_War_II_propaganda_production" title="Walt Disney's World War II propaganda production">to produce training and propaganda films</a>. By 1942, 90% of its 550 employees were working on war-related films.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[14]</a></sup> Films such as the feature <i><a href="/wiki/Victory_Through_Air_Power_(film)" title="Victory Through Air Power (film)">Victory Through Air Power</a></i> and the short <i><a href="/wiki/Education_for_Death" title="Education for Death">Education for Death</a></i> (both 1943) were meant to increase public support for the war effort. Even the studio's characters joined the effort, as <a href="/wiki/Donald_Duck" title="Donald Duck">Donald Duck</a> appeared in a number of comical propaganda shorts, including the Academy Award-winning <i><a href="/wiki/Der_Fuehrer%27s_Face" title="Der Fuehrer's Face">Der Fuehrer's Face</a></i> (1943).
</p><p>With limited staff and little operating capital during and after the war, Disney's feature films during much of the 1940s were "package films", or collections of shorts, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three_Caballeros" title="The Three Caballeros">The Three Caballeros</a></i> (1944) and <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Time" title="Melody Time">Melody Time</a></i> (1948), which performed poorly at the box office. At the same time, the studio began producing live-action films and documentaries. <i><a href="/wiki/Song_of_the_South" title="Song of the South">Song of the South</a></i> (1946) and <i><a href="/wiki/So_Dear_to_My_Heart" title="So Dear to My Heart">So Dear to My Heart</a></i> (1948) featured animated segments, while the <i><a href="/wiki/True-Life_Adventures" title="True-Life Adventures">True-Life Adventures</a></i> series, which included such films as <i><a href="/wiki/Seal_Island_(film)" title="Seal Island (film)">Seal Island</a></i> (1948) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vanishing_Prairie" title="The Vanishing Prairie">The Vanishing Prairie</a></i> (1954), were also popular. Eight of the films in the series won Academy Awards.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1950.E2.80.931966:_Television.2C_Disneyland.2C_and_Walt_Disney.27s_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950–1966:_Television,_Disneyland,_and_Walt_Disney's_death">1950–1966: Television, Disneyland, and Walt Disney's death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1950–1966: Television, Disneyland, and Walt Disney's death" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The release of <i><a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(1950_film)" title="Cinderella (1950 film)">Cinderella</a></i> in 1950 proved that feature-length animation could still succeed in the marketplace. Other releases of the period included <i><a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Wonderland_(1951_film)" title="Alice in Wonderland (1951 film)">Alice in Wonderland</a></i> (1951) and <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_(1953_film)" title="Peter Pan (1953 film)">Peter Pan</a></i> (1953), both in production before the war began, and Disney's first all-live action feature, <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island_(1950_film)" title="Treasure Island (1950 film)">Treasure Island</a></i> (1950). Other early all-live-action Disney films included <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Robin_Hood_and_His_Merrie_Men" title="The Story of Robin Hood and His Merrie Men">The Story of Robin Hood and His Merrie Men</a></i> (1952), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sword_and_the_Rose" title="The Sword and the Rose">The Sword and the Rose</a></i> (1953), and <i><a href="/wiki/20,000_Leagues_Under_the_Sea_(1954_film)" title="20,000 Leagues Under the Sea (1954 film)">20,000 Leagues Under the Sea</a></i> (1954). Disney ended its distribution contract with RKO in 1953, forming its own distribution arm, <a href="/wiki/Buena_Vista_Pictures_Distribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Buena Vista Pictures Distribution">Buena Vista Distribution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-24">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waltopening.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Waltopening.jpg/200px-Waltopening.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="183" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="334" data-file-height="305"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 183px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Waltopening.jpg/200px-Waltopening.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="183" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waltopening.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walt Disney at the grand opening of <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a>, July 1955.</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1950, Walt Disney Productions and <a href="/wiki/The_Coca-Cola_Company" title="The Coca-Cola Company">the Coca-Cola Company</a> teamed up for Disney's first venture into television, the <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> television network special <i>One Hour in Wonderland</i>. In October 1954, the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> network launched Disney's first regular television series. In 1954, Walt Disney used his <i>Disneyland</i> series to unveil what would become <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a>, an idea conceived out of a desire for a place where parents and children could both have fun at the same time. On July 18, 1955, Walt Disney opened Disneyland to the general public. On July 17, 1955, Disneyland was previewed with a live television broadcast hosted by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cummings" title="Robert Cummings">Robert Cummings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_Linkletter" title="Art Linkletter">Art Linkletter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>. After a shaky start, Disneyland continued to grow and attract visitors from across the country and around the world. A major expansion in 1959 included the addition of America's first <a href="/wiki/Disneyland_Monorail_System" title="Disneyland Monorail System">monorail system</a>. For the <a href="/wiki/1964_New_York_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 New York World's Fair">1964 New York World's Fair</a>, Disney prepared four separate attractions for various sponsors, each of which would find its way to Disneyland in one form or another. During this time, Walt Disney was also secretly scouting out new sites for a second Disney theme park. In November 1965, "Disney World" was announced, with plans for theme parks, hotels, and even a model city on thousands of acres of land purchased outside of <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>, Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[17]</a></sup></p><p>Disney continued to focus its talents on television throughout the 1950s. Its weekday afternoon children's television program <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mickey_Mouse_Club" title="The Mickey Mouse Club">The Mickey Mouse Club</a></i>, featuring its roster of young "Mouseketeers", premiered in 1955 to great success, as did the <i><a href="/wiki/Davy_Crockett_(miniseries)" title="Davy Crockett (miniseries)">Davy Crockett</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Fess_Parker" title="Fess Parker">Fess Parker</a> and broadcast on the <i>Disneyland</i> anthology show.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Two years later, the <i><a href="/wiki/Zorro_(1957_TV_series)" title="Zorro (1957 TV series)">Zorro</a></i> series would prove just as popular, running for two seasons on ABC.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[18]</a></sup> Despite such success, Walt Disney Productions invested little into television ventures in the 1960s,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> with the exception of the long-running anthology series, later known as <i>The Wonderful World of Disney</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>Disney's film studios stayed busy as well, averaging five or six releases per year during this period. While the production of shorts slowed significantly during the 1950s and 1960s, the studio released a number of popular animated features, like <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_and_the_Tramp" title="Lady and the Tramp">Lady and the Tramp</a></i> (1955), <i><a href="/wiki/Sleeping_Beauty_(1959_film)" title="Sleeping Beauty (1959 film)">Sleeping Beauty</a></i> (1959) and <i><a href="/wiki/One_Hundred_and_One_Dalmatians" title="One Hundred and One Dalmatians">One Hundred and One Dalmatians</a></i> (1961), which introduced a new <a href="/wiki/Xerography#Uses_in_animation" title="Xerography">xerography</a> process to transfer the drawings to <a href="/wiki/Cel" title="Cel">animation cels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[19]</a></sup> Disney's live-action releases were spread across a number of genres, including historical fiction (<i><a href="/wiki/Johnny_Tremain_(film)" title="Johnny Tremain (film)">Johnny Tremain</a></i>, 1957), adaptations of children's books (<i><a href="/wiki/Pollyanna_(1960_film)" title="Pollyanna (1960 film)">Pollyanna</a></i>, 1960) and modern-day comedies (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Shaggy_Dog_(1959_film)" title="The Shaggy Dog (1959 film)">The Shaggy Dog</a></i>, 1959). Disney's most successful film of the 1960s was a live action/animated musical adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Poppins_(film)" title="Mary Poppins (film)">Mary Poppins</a></i>, which was one of the all-time highest-grossing movies<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> and received five <a href="/wiki/37th_Academy_Awards" title="37th Academy Awards">Academy Awards</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Actress" title="Academy Award for Best Actress">Best Actress</a> for <a href="/wiki/Julie_Andrews" title="Julie Andrews">Julie Andrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Best Song</a> for <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Brothers" title="Sherman Brothers">Robert B. Sherman &amp; Richard M. Sherman</a> for "<a href="/wiki/Chim_Chim_Cher-ee" title="Chim Chim Cher-ee">Chim Chim Cher-ee</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[20]</a></sup> The theme park design and architectural group became so integral to the Disney studio's operations that the studio bought it on February 5, 1965, along with the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Imagineering" title="Walt Disney Imagineering">WED Enterprises</a> name.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[24]</a></sup> On December 15, 1966, Walt Disney died of complications relating to lung cancer,<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> marking the end of an era for the company.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1966.E2.80.931984:_Roy_O._Disney.27s_leadership_and_death.2C_Walt_Disney_World.2C_Tatum.2C_Walker.2C_and_Miller.27s_leaderships.2C_and_theatrical_malaise"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1966–1984:_Roy_O._Disney's_leadership_and_death,_Walt_Disney_World,_Tatum,_Walker,_and_Miller's_leaderships,_and_theatrical_malaise">1966–1984: Roy O. Disney's leadership and death, Walt Disney World, Tatum, Walker, and Miller's leaderships, and theatrical malaise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1966–1984: Roy O. Disney's leadership and death, Walt Disney World, Tatum, Walker, and Miller's leaderships, and theatrical malaise" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Following Walt's death, <a href="/wiki/Roy_O._Disney" title="Roy O. Disney">Roy O. Disney</a> took over as chairman, CEO, and president of the company. One of his first acts was to rename Disney World as "Walt Disney World" in honor of his brother and his vision.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[25]</a></sup> In 1967, the last two films Walt actively supervised were released, the animated feature <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jungle_Book_(1967_film)" title="The Jungle Book (1967 film)">The Jungle Book</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> and the musical <i><a href="/wiki/The_Happiest_Millionaire" title="The Happiest Millionaire">The Happiest Millionaire</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[26]</a></sup> The studio released a number of comedies in the late 1960s, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Love_Bug" title="The Love Bug">The Love Bug</a></i> (1969's highest-grossing film)<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Computer_Wore_Tennis_Shoes" title="The Computer Wore Tennis Shoes">The Computer Wore Tennis Shoes</a></i> (1969), which starred another young Disney discovery, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Russell" title="Kurt Russell">Kurt Russell</a>. The 1970s opened with the release of Disney's first "post-Walt" animated feature, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Aristocats" title="The Aristocats">The Aristocats</a></i>, followed by a return to fantasy musicals in 1971's <i><a href="/wiki/Bedknobs_and_Broomsticks" title="Bedknobs and Broomsticks">Bedknobs and Broomsticks</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Blackbeard%27s_Ghost" title="Blackbeard's Ghost">Blackbeard's Ghost</a></i> was another successful film during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> On October 1, 1971, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_World" title="Walt Disney World">Walt Disney World</a> opened to the public, with Roy Disney dedicating the facility in person later that month.
</p><p>On December 20, 1971, Roy O. Disney died of a stroke. He left the company under the control of <a href="/wiki/Donn_Tatum" title="Donn Tatum">Donn Tatum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Card_Walker" title="Card Walker">Card Walker</a>, and Walt's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Ron_W._Miller" title="Ron W. Miller">Ron Miller</a>, each trained by Walt and Roy. While Walt Disney Productions continued releasing family-friendly films throughout the 1970s, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Escape_to_Witch_Mountain_(1975_film)" title="Escape to Witch Mountain (1975 film)">Escape to Witch Mountain</a></i> (1975)<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Freaky_Friday_(1976_film)" title="Freaky Friday (1976 film)">Freaky Friday</a></i> (1976), the films did not fare as well at the box office as earlier material. However, the animation studio saw success with <i><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood_(1973_film)" title="Robin Hood (1973 film)">Robin Hood</a></i> (1973), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rescuers" title="The Rescuers">The Rescuers</a></i> (1977), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fox_and_the_Hound" title="The Fox and the Hound">The Fox and the Hound</a></i> (1981). As head of the studio, Miller attempted to make films to drive the profitable teenage market who generally passed on seeing Disney films.<sup id="cite_ref-rm_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rm-35">[27]</a></sup> Inspired by the popularity of <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i>, Disney produced the science-fiction adventure <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Hole" title="The Black Hole">The Black Hole</a></i> in 1979; it cost $20 million to make, but was lost in <i>Star Wars'</i> wake.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> <i>The Black Hole</i> was the first Disney film to carry a <a href="/wiki/Motion_Picture_Association_of_America_film_rating_system" title="Motion Picture Association of America film rating system">PG</a> rating in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-rm_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rm-35">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[N 1]</a></sup> Disney dabbled in the horror genre with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Watcher_in_the_Woods_(1980_film)" title="The Watcher in the Woods (1980 film)">The Watcher in the Woods</a></i>, and financed the boldly innovative <i><a href="/wiki/Tron" title="Tron">Tron</a></i>; both films were released to minimal success.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>Disney also hired outside producers for film projects, which had never been done before in the studio's history.<sup id="cite_ref-rm_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rm-35">[27]</a></sup> In 1979, Disney entered a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a> on the production of the 1980 film adaptation of <i><a href="/wiki/Popeye_(film)" title="Popeye (film)">Popeye</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dragonslayer_(1981_film)" title="Dragonslayer (1981 film)">Dragonslayer</a></i> (1981); the first time Disney collaborated with another studio. Paramount distributed Disney films in Canada at the time, and it was hoped that Disney's marketing prestige would help sell the two films.<sup id="cite_ref-rm_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rm-35">[27]</a></sup> Finally, in 1982, the Disney family sold the naming rights and rail-based attractions to the Disney film studio for 818,461 shares of Disney stock then worth $42.6 million none of which went to <a href="/wiki/Retlaw_Enterprises" title="Retlaw Enterprises">Retlaw</a>. Also, <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a> objected to the overvalued purchase price of the naming right and voted against the purchase as a Disney board director.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[28]</a></sup></p><p>The 1983 release of <i><a href="/wiki/Mickey%27s_Christmas_Carol" title="Mickey's Christmas Carol">Mickey's Christmas Carol</a></i> began a string of successful movies, starting with <i><a href="/wiki/Never_Cry_Wolf_(film)" title="Never Cry Wolf (film)">Never Cry Wolf</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Ray_Bradbury" title="Ray Bradbury">Ray Bradbury</a> adaptation <i><a href="/wiki/Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes_(film)" title="Something Wicked This Way Comes (film)">Something Wicked This Way Comes</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The Walt Disney Productions film division was incorporated on <span data-sort-value="000000001983-04-01-0000" style="white-space:nowrap">April 1, 1983</span> as <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[29]</a></sup> In 1984, Disney CEO Ron Miller created <a href="/wiki/Touchstone_Pictures" title="Touchstone Pictures">Touchstone Films</a> as a brand for Disney to release more major motion pictures. Touchstone's first release was the comedy <i><a href="/wiki/Splash_(film)" title="Splash (film)">Splash</a></i> (1984), which was a box office success.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[30]</a></sup> With <i>The Wonderful World of Disney</i> remaining a prime-time staple, Disney returned to television in the 1970s with syndicated programming such as the anthology series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mouse_Factory" title="The Mouse Factory">The Mouse Factory</a></i> and a brief revival of the <i>Mickey Mouse Club</i>. In 1980, Disney launched Walt Disney Home Video to take advantage of the newly emerging videocassette market. On April 18, 1983, <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">The Disney Channel</a> debuted as a subscription-level channel on cable systems nationwide, featuring its large library of classic films and TV series, along with original programming and family-friendly third-party offerings.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG/220px-1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 146px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG/220px-1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="146" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1_epcot_spaceship_earth_2010a.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Epcot" title="Epcot">Epcot</a> opened in October 1982.</div></div></div>
<p>Walt Disney World received much of the company's attention through the 1970s and into the 1980s. In 1978, Disney executives announced plans for the second Walt Disney World theme park, <a href="/wiki/Epcot" title="Epcot">EPCOT Center</a>, which would open in October 1982. Inspired by Walt Disney's dream of a futuristic model city, EPCOT Center was built as a "permanent World's Fair", complete with exhibits sponsored by major American corporations, as well as pavilions based on the cultures of other nations. In Japan, <a href="/wiki/The_Oriental_Land_Company" title="The Oriental Land Company">The Oriental Land Company</a> partnered with Walt Disney Productions to build the first Disney theme park outside of the United States, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Disneyland" title="Tokyo Disneyland">Tokyo Disneyland</a>, which opened in April 1983. Despite the success of the Disney Channel and its new theme park creations, Walt Disney Productions was financially vulnerable. Its film library was valuable, but offered few current successes, and its leadership team was unable to keep up with other studios, particularly the works of <a href="/wiki/Don_Bluth" title="Don Bluth">Don Bluth</a>, who defected from Disney in 1979. By the early 1980s, the parks were generating 70% of Disney's income.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>In 1984, financier <a href="/wiki/Saul_Steinberg_(businessman)" title="Saul Steinberg (businessman)">Saul Steinberg</a>'s Reliance Group Holdings launched a <a href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" class="mw-redirect" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeover</a> bid for Walt Disney Productions,<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> with the intent of selling off some of its operations.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[31]</a></sup> Disney bought out Reliance's 11.1% stake in the company. However, another shareholder filed suit claiming the deal devaluated Disney's stock and for Disney management to retain their positions. The shareholder lawsuit was settled in 1989 for a total of $45 million from Disney and Reliance.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Likewise in 1984, <a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA</a> (then-parent company of <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Studios</a>) actually struck a deal with Disney to purchase the company on the condition insisted by the <a href="/wiki/Disney_family" title="Disney family">Disney family</a> that Disney CEO <a href="/wiki/Ron_W._Miller" title="Ron W. Miller">Ron W. Miller</a> be MCA president, but disagreements between MCA chairman <a href="/wiki/Lew_Wasserman" title="Lew Wasserman">Lew Wasserman</a> and Disney over the condition caused the agreement to fall through completely.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1984.E2.80.932005:_Michael_Eisner.27s_leadership.2C_Disney_Renaissance.2C_and_.22Save_Disney.22_campaign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1984–2005:_Michael_Eisner's_leadership,_Disney_Renaissance,_and_&quot;Save_Disney&quot;_campaign">1984–2005: Michael Eisner's leadership, Disney Renaissance, and "Save Disney" campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1984–2005: Michael Eisner's leadership, Disney Renaissance, and &quot;Save Disney&quot; campaign" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_The_Walt_Disney_Company#1984%E2%80%932004" title="Timeline of The Walt Disney Company">Timeline of The Walt Disney Company § 1984–2004</a></div>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Sid_Bass" title="Sid Bass">Sid Bass</a> family purchase of 18.7 percent of Disney, Bass and the board brought in <a href="/wiki/Michael_Eisner" title="Michael Eisner">Michael Eisner</a> from Paramount as CEO and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wells" title="Frank Wells">Frank Wells</a> from <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> as president. Eisner emphasized Touchstone, with <i><a href="/wiki/Down_and_Out_in_Beverly_Hills" title="Down and Out in Beverly Hills">Down and Out in Beverly Hills</a></i> (1985) leading to increased output with <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Morning,_Vietnam" title="Good Morning, Vietnam">Good Morning, Vietnam</a></i> (1987), <i><a href="/wiki/Dead_Poets_Society" title="Dead Poets Society">Dead Poets Society</a></i> (1989), <i><a href="/wiki/Pretty_Woman" title="Pretty Woman">Pretty Woman</a></i> (1990) and additional hits. Eisner used expanding cable and home video markets to sign deals using Disney shows and films, making a long-term deal with <a href="/wiki/Showtime_Networks" title="Showtime Networks">Showtime Networks</a> for Disney/Touchstone releases through 1996 and entering television with syndication and distribution for TV series such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Golden_Girls" title="The Golden Girls">The Golden Girls</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Home_Improvement_(TV_series)" title="Home Improvement (TV series)">Home Improvement</a></i>. Disney began limited releases of its previous films on video tapes in the late 1980s. Eisner's Disney purchased <a href="/wiki/KCAL-TV" title="KCAL-TV">KHJ</a>, an independent Los Angeles TV station.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Organized in 1985, <a href="/wiki/Silver_Screen_Partners" title="Silver Screen Partners">Silver Screen Partners</a> II, LP financed films for Disney with $193 million. In January 1987, Silver Screen III began financing movies for Disney with $300 million raised, the largest amount raised for a film financing limited partnership by E.F. Hutton.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[33]</a></sup> Silver Screen IV was also set up to finance Disney's studios.<sup id="cite_ref-lat_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lat-43">[34]</a></sup></p><p>Buoyed by the success of <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Framed_Roger_Rabbit" title="Who Framed Roger Rabbit">Who Framed Roger Rabbit</a></i> in 1988, Disney's <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">flagship animation studio</a> enjoyed a series of commercial and critical successes known as the <a href="/wiki/Disney_Renaissance" title="Disney Renaissance">Disney Renaissance</a>, with such films as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Mermaid_(1989_film)" title="The Little Mermaid (1989 film)">The Little Mermaid</a></i> (1989), <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> (1991), <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i> (1992), and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i> (1994). In addition, the company successfully entered the field of television animation with a number of lavishly-budgeted and acclaimed series such as <i><a href="/wiki/Disney%27s_Adventures_of_the_Gummi_Bears" title="Disney's Adventures of the Gummi Bears">Adventures of the Gummi Bears</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/DuckTales" title="DuckTales">DuckTales</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chip_%27n_Dale:_Rescue_Rangers" title="Chip 'n Dale: Rescue Rangers">Chip 'n Dale: Rescue Rangers</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Darkwing_Duck" title="Darkwing Duck">Darkwing Duck</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/TaleSpin" title="TaleSpin">TaleSpin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bonkers_(TV_series)" title="Bonkers (TV series)">Bonkers</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gargoyles_(TV_series)" title="Gargoyles (TV series)">Gargoyles</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[35]</a></sup> Disney moved to first place in box office receipts by 1988 and had increased revenues by 20% every year.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>In 1989, Disney signed an agreement-in-principle to acquire <a href="/wiki/The_Jim_Henson_Company" title="The Jim Henson Company">The Jim Henson Company</a> from its founder, <a href="/wiki/The_Muppets" title="The Muppets">Muppet</a> creator <a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>. The deal included Henson's programming library and Muppet characters (excluding the Muppets created for <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i>), as well as Jim Henson's personal creative services. However, Henson died suddenly in May 1990 before the deal was completed, resulting in the two companies terminating <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">merger</a> negotiations the following December.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[36]</a></sup> Named the "Disney Decade" by the company, the executive talent attempted to move the company to new heights in the 1990s with huge changes and accomplishments.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> In September 1990, Disney arranged for financing up to $200 million by a unit of <a href="/wiki/Nomura_Securities" title="Nomura Securities">Nomura Securities</a> for <a href="/wiki/Interscope_Communications" title="Interscope Communications">Interscope</a> films made for Disney. On October 23, Disney formed <a href="/wiki/Touchwood_Pacific_Partners" title="Touchwood Pacific Partners">Touchwood Pacific Partners</a> which would supplant the Silver Screen Partnership series as their movie studios' primary source of funding.<sup id="cite_ref-lat_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lat-43">[34]</a></sup></p><p>In 1991, hotels, home video distribution, and Disney merchandising became 28% of total company revenues while international revenues contributed 22% of total revenues. The company committed its studios in the first quarter of 1991 to produce 25 films in 1992. However, 1991 saw net income drop by 23% and had no growth for the year, but saw the release of <i>Beauty and the Beast</i>, winner of two Academy Awards and top-grossing film in the genre. Disney next moved into publishing with <a href="/wiki/Hachette_Books" title="Hachette Books">Hyperion Books</a> and adult music with <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Records" title="Hollywood Records">Hollywood Records</a> while <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Imagineering" title="Walt Disney Imagineering">Walt Disney Imagineering</a> was laying off 400 employees.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Disney also broadened its adult offerings in film when then-Disney Studio Chairman <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Katzenberg" title="Jeffrey Katzenberg">Jeffrey Katzenberg</a> acquired <a href="/wiki/Miramax" title="Miramax">Miramax Films</a> in 1993. That same year Disney created the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">NHL</a> team the <a href="/wiki/Anaheim_Ducks" title="Anaheim Ducks">Mighty Ducks of Anaheim</a>, named after the <a href="/wiki/The_Mighty_Ducks" title="The Mighty Ducks">1992 hit film of the same name</a>. Disney purchased a minority stake in the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Angels" title="Los Angeles Angels">Anaheim Angels</a> baseball team around the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>Wells was killed in a helicopter crash in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Shortly thereafter, Katzenberg resigned and formed <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Pictures" title="DreamWorks Pictures">DreamWorks SKG</a> because Eisner would not appoint Katzenberg to Wells' now-available post (Katzenberg had also sued over the terms of his contract).<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Instead, Eisner recruited his friend <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ovitz" title="Michael Ovitz">Michael Ovitz</a>, one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Creative_Artists_Agency" title="Creative Artists Agency">Creative Artists Agency</a>, to be President, with minimal involvement from Disney's board of directors (which at the time included Oscar-winning actor <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Poitier" title="Sidney Poitier">Sidney Poitier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hilton_Worldwide" title="Hilton Worldwide">Hilton Hotels Corporation</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bollenbach" title="Stephen Bollenbach">Stephen Bollenbach</a>, former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senator</a> <a href="/wiki/George_J._Mitchell" title="George J. Mitchell">George Mitchell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Architecture" title="Yale School of Architecture">Yale</a> dean <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._M._Stern" title="Robert A. M. Stern">Robert A. M. Stern</a>, and Eisner's predecessors <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Watson" title="Raymond Watson">Raymond Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Card_Walker" title="Card Walker">Card Walker</a>). Ovitz lasted only 14 months and left Disney in December 1996 via a "no fault termination" with a <a href="/wiki/Severance_package" title="Severance package">severance package</a> of $38 million in cash and 3 million <a href="/wiki/Employee_stock_option" title="Employee stock option">stock options</a> worth roughly $100 million at the time of Ovitz's departure. The Ovitz episode engendered a long-running <a href="/wiki/Derivative_suit" title="Derivative suit">derivative suit</a>, which finally concluded in June 2006, almost 10 years later. Chancellor <a href="/wiki/William_B._Chandler_III" title="William B. Chandler III">William B. Chandler III</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Court_of_Chancery" title="Delaware Court of Chancery">Delaware Court of Chancery</a>, despite describing Eisner's behavior as falling "far short of what shareholders expect and demand from those entrusted with a <a href="/wiki/Fiduciary" title="Fiduciary">fiduciary</a> position..." found in favor of Eisner and the rest of the Disney board because they had not violated the letter of the law (namely, the <a href="/wiki/Duty_of_care" title="Duty of care">duty of care</a> owed by a corporation's officers and board to its shareholders).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[37]</a></sup> Eisner later said, in a 2016 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Reporter" title="The Hollywood Reporter">The Hollywood Reporter</a></i>, that he regretted letting Ovitz go.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="Celebration Florida" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg/400px-022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg" decoding="async" width="400" height="120" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="679"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 400px;height: 120px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg/400px-022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg" data-alt="Celebration Florida" data-width="400" data-height="120" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:022306-CelebrationFL01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of downtown <a href="/wiki/Celebration,_Florida" title="Celebration, Florida">Celebration, Florida</a>, a community that was planned by the Walt Disney Company.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1994, Eisner attempted to purchase <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> from <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE), but the deal failed due to GE wanting to keep 51% ownership of the network. On August 1, 1995, Disney announced they would acquire and merge with <a href="/wiki/Capital_Cities/ABC_Inc." title="Capital Cities/ABC Inc.">Capital Cities/ABC Inc.</a> for $19 billion, which at the time was the second largest corporate takeover. The merger would bring broadcast network <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> and its assets, including a 37.5% minority stake in <a href="/wiki/A%2BE_Networks" class="mw-redirect" title="A+E Networks">A&amp;E Television Networks</a>, an 80% majority stake in <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> and the Limited Partnership-ran <a href="/wiki/DIC_Productions" class="mw-redirect" title="DIC Productions">DIC Productions</a> into the Disney umberella.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The deal was closed on February 10, 1996, and Eisner felt that the purchase of ABC was an important investment to keep Disney surviving and allowing it to compete with international multimedia conglomerates.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[39]</a></sup> Disney lost a $10.4 million lawsuit in September 1997 to Marsu B.V. over Disney's failure to produce as contracted 13 half-hour <i><a href="/wiki/Marsupilami" title="Marsupilami">Marsupilami</a></i> cartoon shows. Instead, Disney felt other internal "hot properties" deserved the company's attention.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[40]</a></sup></p><p>Disney, which had taken control of the Anaheim Angels in 1996, purchased a majority stake in the team in 1998. That same year, Disney began a move into the internet field with the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Starwave" title="Starwave">Starwave</a> and 43% of <a href="/wiki/Infoseek" title="Infoseek">Infoseek</a>. In 1999, Disney purchased the remaining shares of Infoseek and launched the <a href="/wiki/Go.com" title="Go.com">Go Network</a> portal in January. Disney also launched its <a href="/wiki/Disney_Cruise_Line" title="Disney Cruise Line">cruise line</a> with the christening of <i><a href="/wiki/Disney_Magic" title="Disney Magic">Disney Magic</a></i> and a sister ship, <i><a href="/wiki/Disney_Wonder" title="Disney Wonder">Disney Wonder</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The Katzenberg case dragged on as his contract included a portion of the film revenue from ancillary markets forever. Katzenberg had offered $100 million to settle the case, but Eisner felt the original claim amount of about half a billion too much, but then the ancillary market clause was found. Disney lawyers tried to indicate a decline situation which reveal some of the problems in the company. ABC had declining rating and increasing costs while the film segment had two film failures. While neither party revealed the settlement amount, it is estimated at $200 million.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Disney_Magic2.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Disney_Magic2.jpg/280px-Disney_Magic2.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="200" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1599" data-file-height="1143"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 280px;height: 200px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Disney_Magic2.jpg/280px-Disney_Magic2.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="280" data-height="200" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Disney_Magic2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Disney_Magic" title="Disney Magic">Disney Magic</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Disney_Cruise_Line" title="Disney Cruise Line">Disney Cruise Line</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cabo_San_Lucas" title="Cabo San Lucas">Cabo San Lucas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Eisner's controlling style inhibited efficiency and progress according to some critics, while other industry experts indicated that "age compression" theory led to a decline in the company's target market due to youth copying teenage behavior earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> The year 2000 brought an increase in revenue of 9% and net income of 39% with ABC and ESPN leading the way and Parks and Resorts marking its sixth consecutive year of growth. In November 2000, <a href="/wiki/Andy_Heyward" title="Andy Heyward">Andy Heyward</a> purchased back <a href="/wiki/DIC_Entertainment" title="DIC Entertainment">DIC Entertainment</a> from Disney (through investment by <a href="/wiki/Bain_Capital" title="Bain Capital">Bain Capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chase_Capital_Partners" class="mw-redirect" title="Chase Capital Partners">Chase Capital Partners</a>) and making the studio re-independent.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[41]</a></sup> On July 23, 2001, Disney announced to purchase <a href="/wiki/ABC_Family_Worldwide" title="ABC Family Worldwide">Fox Family Worldwide</a> for $2.9 billion cash plus $2.3 billion in debt assumption, which would include ownership in the <a href="/wiki/Fox_Family_Channel" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox Family Channel">Fox Family</a> channel alongside other assets including the <a href="/wiki/Saban_Entertainment" title="Saban Entertainment">Saban Entertainment</a> library and <a href="/wiki/Fox_Kids" title="Fox Kids">Fox Kids</a> channels in Europe and Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[42]</a></sup> The purchase was completed on October 24, 2001 and Fox Family would be renamed to <a href="/wiki/ABC_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="ABC Family">ABC Family</a> in November.
</p><p>The year 2001 was one of cost cutting, laying off 4,000 employees, Disney parks operations decreased, slashing annual live-action film investment, and minimizing Internet operations, mainly due to the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, which led to a decline in vacation travel and the <a href="/wiki/Early_2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Early 2000s recession">early 2000s recession</a> led to a decrease in ABC revenue. While 2002 revenue had a small decrease from 2001 with the cost cutting, net income rose to $1.2 billion with two creative film releases. In 2003, Disney became the first studio to record over $3 billion in worldwide box office receipts.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> Eisner did not want the board to renominate <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a>, the son of Disney co-founder <a href="/wiki/Roy_O._Disney" title="Roy O. Disney">Roy O. Disney</a>, as a board director citing his age of 72 as a required retirement age. <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Gold" title="Stanley Gold">Stanley Gold</a> responded by resigning from the board and requesting the other board members oust Eisner.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> On November 30, 2003, Disney resigned from his positions as the company's vice chairman and chairman of <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Feature Animation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-y03_group=ChWDC_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y03_group=ChWDC-52">[ChWDC 9]</a></sup> accusing Eisner of <a href="/wiki/Micromanagement" title="Micromanagement">micromanagement</a>, failures with the ABC television network, timidity in the <a href="/wiki/Amusement_park" title="Amusement park">theme park</a> business, turning The Walt Disney Company into a "rapacious, soul-less" company, and refusing to establish a clear succession plan, as well as a string of box office film flops starting in the year 2000.
</p><p>On August 9, 2002, Disney said it was expressing great interest in buying <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Studios</a> whose parent company <a href="/wiki/Vivendi" title="Vivendi">Vivendi</a> started a bidding war after inheriting $17.9 billion in debt by its purchase of the famed <a href="/wiki/Major_film_studio" title="Major film studio">major film studio</a> from <a href="/wiki/Seagram" title="Seagram">Seagram</a> for $34 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-GTG_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GTG-53">[43]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Universal_Orlando" title="Universal Orlando">Universal Orlando</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Islands_of_Adventure" class="mw-redirect" title="Islands of Adventure">Islands of Adventure</a> was struggling to deal with catastrophically low attendance since the park's opening in 1999, and the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001 caused a dip of <a href="/wiki/Universal_Parks_and_Resorts" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal Parks and Resorts">Universal Parks and Resorts</a>' tourism attendance worldwide. As a result, Vivendi lacked the interest in investing in the Universal parks more meaningfully and may have been one of the reasons for selling off Universal.<sup id="cite_ref-FEAD_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEAD-54">[44]</a></sup> Analysts speculated that Universal would have to be available at a bargain price to justify such a deal. "Owning more theme parks could make Disney even more cyclical because that's a cyclical business," said Katherine Styponias of <a href="/wiki/Prudential_Securities" title="Prudential Securities">Prudential Securities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GTG_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GTG-53">[43]</a></sup> Despite this, Disney didn't succeed in pursuing a takeover for various reasons, owing to its stock price at a 52-week-low and the likelihood of the Disney/Universal deal being blocked on antitrust grounds (e.g. less innovation in theme parks, higher prices for hotel rooms, growing power of box office market share, etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-FEAD_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEAD-54">[44]</a></sup></p><p>On May 15, 2003, Disney sold their stake in the Anaheim Angels baseball team to <a href="/wiki/Arte_Moreno" title="Arte Moreno">Arte Moreno</a>. Disney purchased the rights to <a href="/wiki/The_Muppets" title="The Muppets">The Muppets</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Bear_in_the_Big_Blue_House" title="Bear in the Big Blue House">Bear in the Big Blue House</a></i> franchises from <a href="/wiki/The_Jim_Henson_Company" title="The Jim Henson Company">The Jim Henson Company</a> on February 17, 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Muppets_2004_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muppets_2004-55">[45]</a></sup> The two brands were placed under control of the <a href="/wiki/The_Muppets_Studio" title="The Muppets Studio">Muppets Holding Company, LLC</a>, a unit of <a href="/wiki/Disney_Consumer_Products" title="Disney Consumer Products">Disney Consumer Products</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-THRMuppets_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THRMuppets-56">[46]</a></sup> In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar Animation Studios</a> began looking for another distributor after its 12-year contract with Disney ended, due to its strained relationship over issues of control and money with Eisner. Also that year, <a href="/wiki/Comcast" title="Comcast">Comcast</a> Corporation made an unsolicited $54 billion bid to acquire Disney. A couple of high budget films flopped at the box office. With these difficulties and with some board directors dissatisfied, Eisner ceded the board chairmanship.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup></p><p>On March 3, 2004, at Disney's annual shareholders' meeting, a surprising 45% of Disney's shareholders, predominantly rallied by former board members Roy Disney and Stanley Gold, withheld their <a href="/wiki/Proxy_voting" title="Proxy voting">proxies</a> to re-elect Eisner to the board. Disney's board then gave the chairmanship position to Mitchell. However, the board did not immediately remove Eisner as chief executive.<sup id="cite_ref-y04_group=ChWDC_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y04_group=ChWDC-57">[ChWDC 10]</a></sup> In February 2005, Disney sold the <a href="/wiki/Anaheim_Ducks" title="Anaheim Ducks">Mighty Ducks of Anaheim</a> hockey team to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Samueli" title="Henry Samueli">Henry and Susan Samueli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fu_8-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fu-8">[7]</a></sup> On March 13, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Iger" title="Bob Iger">Robert A. Iger</a> was announced as Eisner's successor as CEO. Also that month, Miramax co-founders <a href="/wiki/Bob_Weinstein" title="Bob Weinstein">Bob Weinstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Weinstein" title="Harvey Weinstein">Harvey Weinstein</a> departed the company to form their <a href="/wiki/The_Weinstein_Company" title="The Weinstein Company">own studio</a>. On July 8, Walt Disney's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a>, returned to the company as a consultant and as non-voting director emeritus. <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Parks_and_Resorts" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney Parks and Resorts">Walt Disney Parks and Resorts</a> celebrated the 50th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">Disneyland Park</a> on July 17 and opened <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Disneyland" title="Hong Kong Disneyland">Hong Kong Disneyland</a> on September 12. On July 25, Disney announced that it was closing DisneyToon Studios Australia in October 2006 after 17 years of existence.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[47]</a></sup> On September 30, Eisner resigned both as an executive and as a member of the Board of Directors.<sup id="cite_ref-y05_group=ChWDC_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-y05_group=ChWDC-59">[ChWDC 11]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2005.E2.80.93present:_Bob_Iger.27s_leadership_and_company_expansion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2005–present:_Bob_Iger's_leadership_and_company_expansion">2005–present: Bob Iger's leadership and company expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Walt_Disney_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2005–present: Bob Iger's leadership and company expansion" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acquisition_of_21st_Century_Fox_by_Disney" title="Acquisition of 21st Century Fox by Disney">Acquisition of 21st Century Fox by Disney</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg/220px-Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg/220px-Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Teamdisneyburbankbuilding.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Team_Disney" title="Team Disney">Team Disney</a> Burbank, which houses the offices of Disney's CEO and several other senior corporate officials</div></div></div>
<p>On October 1, 2005, Bob Iger replaced Eisner as Disney's CEO. On November 4, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Feature_Animation" class="mw-redirect" title="Walt Disney Feature Animation">Walt Disney Feature Animation</a> released <i><a href="/wiki/Chicken_Little_(2005_film)" title="Chicken Little (2005 film)">Chicken Little</a></i>, the company's first film using 3D animation. On January 23, 2006, it was announced that Disney would purchase Pixar in an all-stock transaction valued at $7.4 billion. The deal was finalized on May 5; <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">Steve Jobs</a>, who was Pixar's CEO and held a 50.1% ownership stake in the company, transitioned to Disney's board of directors as its largest individual shareholder, with a 7% stake.<sup id="cite_ref-PixarAcquisitionNYTimes_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PixarAcquisitionNYTimes-60">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PixarDisneySubsidiary_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PixarDisneySubsidiary-61">[49]</a></sup> Ed Catmull took over as President of Pixar Animation Studios.
